### 2. MA-Modeling-Assets-sales338-Election-vs-Stock-Sales--DTADTLs

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-Assets-sales338-Election-vs-Stock-Sales--DTADTLs/transcript/MA-Modeling-Assets-sales338-Election-vs-Stock-Sales--DTADTLs.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-Assets-sales338-Election-vs-Stock-Sales--DTADTLs\transcript\MA-Modeling-Assets-sales338-Election-vs-Stock-Sales--DTADTLs.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] In the prior video, we introduced the impact of an acquisition on the target's balance sheet.
[00:06 - 00:12] Up until now, the discussion has been about the GAP-based balance sheet.
[00:12 - 00:14] But companies prepare two sets of books.
[00:14 - 00:19] One is in accordance with GAP, generally accepted accounting principles, both either U.S.
[00:19 - 00:21] GAP or IFRS.
[00:21 - 00:27] And the other, with according to tax rules, in other words, the financial statements
[00:27 - 00:33] that are submitted to the United States of the IRS, follow similar but somewhat different
[00:33 - 00:40] accounting rules than the accounting rules that are used for GAP purposes.
[00:40 - 00:46] So depending on how a deal is structured, there can be a major difference between the
[00:46 - 00:54] carrying values of assets as they are accounted for for book purposes versus tax purposes.
[00:54 - 00:58] Broadly speaking, we've laid out a table that identifies the major themes of the marriage
[00:58 - 01:05] or paradigm in M&A, which is from a book perspective, it's what we've been talking about
[01:05 - 01:06] now.
[01:06 - 01:11] Basically, it doesn't really matter legally how the transaction is structured.
[01:11 - 01:16] Any sort of legal structure of a deal is basically accounted for with this write-up to
[01:16 - 01:19] fair market value that we talked about in the prior video.
[01:19 - 01:25] The book values of assets, we call it the book bases, both of assets and liabilities get
[01:25 - 01:27] written up or down to fair market value.
[01:27 - 01:30] Nothing new there, based on what we've been talking about.
[01:30 - 01:34] This just represents the book, the book financials, the ones that we as investors and as
[01:34 - 01:38] bankers have access to through the SEC, et cetera.
[01:38 - 01:45] The tax returns, which are not made public to us, the way that you account, the way
[01:45 - 01:51] that the IRS in the United States sort of accounts for the carrying values of assets actually
[01:51 - 01:58] depends on the legal structure of the transaction, specifically if the deal is structured
[01:58 - 02:09] legally as a stock sale, there is no change for tax purposes in the tax return balance sheet.
[02:09 - 02:13] There's no change in basis for the assets or liabilities, they don't get stepped up.
[02:13 - 02:19] The easiest way to think about it is from a tax perspective, it's as if no acquisition
[02:19 - 02:25] has really taken place that merits the revaluation of the assets.
[02:25 - 02:30] If the deal is structured, however, for legal purposes as an asset sale or what's called
[02:30 - 02:36] a 338-H10 election, which is basically like an asset sale, it's actually a stock sale
[02:36 - 02:39] that for tax purposes is treated as an asset sale.
[02:39 - 02:42] We spend a little bit of time talking about that towards the end of the course.
[02:42 - 02:46] So basically, when we talk about asset sales, we actually really talk about asset sale
[02:46 - 02:47] slash 338s.
[02:47 - 02:54] It just so happens that because it's easier to implement using this construct to actually
[02:54 - 02:58] affect the deal in this construct, most asset sales are actually stock sales that are deemed
[02:58 - 02:59] as it sells under 338.
[02:59 - 03:02] You don't really need to know the difference at this point, other than an asset sale
[03:02 - 03:06] is basically like a 338-H10 election for accounting purposes.
[03:06 - 03:14] And so, when a deal is structured as an asset sale legal or 338, just like for book purposes,
[03:14 - 03:20] the assets are stepped up to fair market value and just some jargon so you understand
[03:20 - 03:24] when we talk about book purposes, assets get written up and we talk about tax purposes
[03:24 - 03:25] that get stepped up.
[03:25 - 03:26] It means the same thing.
[03:26 - 03:29] But when you're talking about tax returns, it's called a step up and you're talking
[03:29 - 03:31] about books, it's called a write up.
[03:31 - 03:36] So what you need to keep in mind is when a deal is structured as an asset sale slash
[03:36 - 03:40] 338, the treatment for tax purposes is basically the same as what we've been talking about.
[03:40 - 03:43] It means there are far fewer headaches for us in modeling.
[03:43 - 03:48] It's not like there are no headaches but there are fewer headaches.
[03:48 - 03:53] Whereas if a deal is structured as a stock sale for book purposes, you now potentially have
[03:53 - 03:59] a huge deviation between now the going forward carrying values of the assets for tax purposes
[03:59 - 04:06] and then now usually much higher value for book purposes.
[04:06 - 04:14] So the purpose of this video is to discuss the accounting and real implications of the
[04:14 - 04:16] differences that we just talked about.
[04:16 - 04:19] Keep in mind the following things.
[04:19 - 04:24] The gap basis is disclosed but the tax basis will likely be unknown to you when you're
[04:24 - 04:25] analyzing a deal.
[04:25 - 04:27] What do I mean by that?
[04:27 - 04:32] If the target has provided you their financial statements or they are a public company,
[04:32 - 04:38] they're providing you the gap basis financials, the book based financials.
[04:38 - 04:44] You rarely get access to the company's tax returns.
[04:44 - 04:50] As a result, the assumptions have to be made about what the tax basis might look like
[04:50 - 04:54] and the framework that we're introducing here provides for just rules of thumb.
[04:54 - 04:56] We're operating with very limited information.
[04:56 - 05:01] So what we're about to talk about as it relates to what the tax balance sheet might
[05:01 - 05:06] look like is really a lot of assumptions and some extent guesswork.
[05:06 - 05:10] So we're giving you some general rules of thumb in the typical context of limited information.
[05:10 - 05:14] This is our sort of disclaimer here.
[05:14 - 05:19] When tax issues are important, you're almost always going to be working alongside qualified
[05:19 - 05:25] tax advisors in a true complete assessment of tax issues should always be in real life
[05:25 - 05:32] for always conducted in consultation with qualified tax advisors.
[05:32 - 05:34] So let's get into it.
[05:34 - 05:40] When deals are structured as asset sales slash 338 H10 elections, the target's book and tax
[05:40 - 05:46] balance sheets both get marked up to fair value with excess getting recorded as goodwill.
[05:46 - 05:48] Exactly like for book purposes.
[05:48 - 05:54] The IRS recognizes that an acquisition is taken place, therefore, meriting the otherwise
[05:54 - 05:59] not allowed revaluation to fair value.
[05:59 - 06:03] And understand that the acquire is very happy about this generally to have a deal structured
[06:03 - 06:05] as an asset sale.
[06:05 - 06:12] Because for tax purposes, this revaluation means that you get to record for tax purposes
[06:12 - 06:18] higher valued assets like property plan equipment and intangible assets, which of course
[06:18 - 06:23] lead to higher future depreciation and amortization, then you would have had had there
[06:23 - 06:28] been no revaluation like what is the case in a stock sale.
[06:28 - 06:33] And so this higher depreciation and amortization expense going forward reduces taxable income
[06:33 - 06:38] and reduces the income that you have to pay taxes on and that creates a very real future
[06:38 - 06:40] tax savings for the acquire.
[06:40 - 06:44] So the acquire generally very much favors an asset sale.
[06:44 - 06:49] There's one other thing that we need to be aware of that makes asset sales extremely
[06:49 - 06:56] attractive for acquires and that is that unlike book accounting, tax accounting actually
[06:56 - 07:00] allows the good will recognize in the acquisition to be amortized.
[07:00 - 07:05] So for tax purposes, you actually amortized goodwill when you recognize it in a NASA
[07:05 - 07:07] sale or 338 election.
[07:07 - 07:13] And that often leads to significant additional future tax savings beyond the DNA and
[07:13 - 07:15] incremental DNA.
[07:15 - 07:18] Just a couple of fine points on that.
[07:18 - 07:23] Good will is amortized over 15 years for tax purposes just like all other intangible assets
[07:23 - 07:26] are amortized over 15 years.
[07:26 - 07:32] So this lays the groundwork for acquires generally very much preferring asset sales.
[07:32 - 07:37] Let's contrast that with when deals are legally structured as stock sales.
[07:37 - 07:41] And just for our purposes now, all I'll say, you can structure a deal legally as either
[07:41 - 07:45] a stock sale or an asset sale to some extent, it ends up being sort of a joint preference
[07:45 - 07:47] by the buyer and the seller.
[07:47 - 07:51] Well, when the deal structure is a stock sale, the target's book balance sheet gets marked
[07:51 - 07:54] up, but the tax balance sheet does not.
[07:54 - 07:58] So now you have this big deviation for book purposes, what the carrying values are and
[07:58 - 08:02] what the carrying values are for tax purposes.
[08:02 - 08:07] But understand that this is mostly a temporary deviation because the assets that get written
[08:07 - 08:13] up for book purposes will be depreciated over time, presumably down to zero or whatever
[08:13 - 08:14] the salvage value is.
[08:14 - 08:18] And we assume that the salvage value will be the same for book purposes as it is for
[08:18 - 08:21] tax purposes.
[08:21 - 08:25] So understand that the deviation that's now created in an easy chart will sort of show
[08:25 - 08:26] this.
[08:26 - 08:34] If you have, let's say you have, you start on the deal date with PPE of 200 and all
[08:34 - 08:42] the sudden you write up for book purposes, the PPE need to say 500.
[08:42 - 08:45] But for tax purposes, you don't get to write it up.
[08:45 - 08:48] And let's say, let's keep a real simple, useful life is, you know, over two years.
[08:48 - 08:54] So it goes from 500, you know, it goes down to a value of zero because it's depreciated
[08:54 - 08:55] over, you know, two years.
[08:55 - 09:02] So you have one and two, this is, this is time, this is number of years.
[09:03 - 09:06] For tax purposes, it also ends up at zero.
[09:06 - 09:07] It's appreciated.
[09:07 - 09:11] Let's assume over two years and it just goes down from 200 to 100 to zero.
[09:11 - 09:19] So this is, this deviation that's created on deal day, this deviation is a temporary one
[09:19 - 09:22] that slowly gets reduced.
[09:22 - 09:26] Gap accounts for this temporary difference between the book and the tax basis of assets
[09:26 - 09:32] and liabilities via the creation on deal day, the day that this difference is created.
[09:32 - 09:38] The accounts for this difference by the creation of deferred tax assets and liabilities.
[09:38 - 09:42] And we're going to talk about what's created here is a deferred tax asset or liability
[09:42 - 09:45] in a second, but just understand that whenever there's a difference in bases between
[09:45 - 09:53] book and tax basis, under U.S. gap, NF and IFRS, you account for it with deferred taxes.
[09:53 - 09:59] There are two major exceptions to this, specifically to the creation deferred taxes to
[09:59 - 10:01] account for the differences.
[10:01 - 10:05] And those, and those exceptions are land in goodwill.
[10:05 - 10:10] So you do get to write up land in goodwill for book purposes, but in a stock sale, you don't
[10:10 - 10:14] get to step them up for tax purposes, but they don't create the for taxes.
[10:14 - 10:19] And that's because neither land nor goodwill are depreciated or amortized for book purposes.
[10:19 - 10:25] Goodwill, as we just talked about is actually amortized when it's recognized for tax purposes,
[10:25 - 10:31] but in a stock sale, it's not recognized and therefore it's not amortized, creating this
[10:31 - 10:32] difference.
[10:32 - 10:36] So goodwill is recognized for book purposes, not for tax purposes.
[10:36 - 10:41] So you have much higher goodwill for book than you have for tax, same with land.
[10:41 - 10:46] But those don't generate any deferred taxes because the difference is temporary.
[10:46 - 10:47] And again, the chart will explain.
[10:47 - 10:49] Let's look at land.
[10:49 - 10:57] If you have land, say of 100, and for book purposes, it's been written up to 300, well,
[10:57 - 11:03] since land doesn't get to appreciate it, it just stays at 300 forever.
[11:03 - 11:08] Meanwhile, for tax purposes in a stock sale, it stays at 100 forever.
[11:08 - 11:11] This difference, unlike this one, does not reverse itself.
[11:11 - 11:18] It stays at this place forever, creating no deferred taxes, it's a permanent difference
[11:18 - 11:23] and no deferred tax is recognized, same with goodwill, same issue exists with goodwill.
[11:23 - 11:27] We're going to get into examples that sort of highlight this, but just keep in mind, in
[11:27 - 11:34] a stock sale, deferred taxes basically capture these differences for book purposes, with exception
[11:34 - 11:37] of land and goodwill.
[11:37 - 11:42] So from the acquires perspective, an asset sale, at least in terms of the future tax
[11:42 - 11:46] savings, is clearly preferable to a stock sale.
[11:46 - 11:51] Just with the stock sale, when you have these differences, there's actually no tax benefit
[11:51 - 11:53] to the acquire.
[11:53 - 11:56] With the stock sale, even though you get to write up assets and you get the incremental
[11:56 - 12:01] depreciation, amortization for book purposes, notice there's nothing actually happening for
[12:01 - 12:02] tax purposes.
[12:02 - 12:05] You don't get the incremental DNA, you don't get the goodwill amortization.
[12:05 - 12:07] So the acquire generally doesn't like this.
[12:07 - 12:13] There's actually a reason that surprisingly stock sales end up making up the majority legal
[12:13 - 12:18] structure in M&A, and that has to do with sort of the target preferences.
[12:18 - 12:22] The target tends to benefit quite a bit from structured deals as stock sales, but we're
[12:22 - 12:24] going to defer that discussion for a little bit later.
[12:24 - 12:29] The main thing that I'd like you to take away at this point is that asset sales create
[12:29 - 12:37] these tax advantages for these asset sales, create advantages for the acquire from future
[12:37 - 12:42] tax savings of incremental DNA and goodwill, and they don't create any deferred tax
[12:42 - 12:47] assets or liabilities because there are no real differences created as opposed to
[12:47 - 12:53] stock sales where there are massive differences that create different taxes between the
[12:53 - 12:54] book and the tax basis.

[00:00 - 00:05] And now let's talk about what represents sort of a rough outline for the buy side process.
[00:05 - 00:09] So if you're advising on the buy side, we're going to talk about the sell side in just a moment,
[00:09 - 00:16] but if you are involved in trying to acquire, trying to provide advisory services for a buyer,
[00:16 - 00:17] your client is a buyer.
[00:17 - 00:18] What does that look like?
[00:18 - 00:23] Well, when the clients, the acquire, the M&A process can roughly be broken out into four
[00:23 - 00:30] stages, one is sort of assessing finding potential targets, second phase is contacting those targets,
[00:30 - 00:34] then pursuing the actual deal and doing what's called due diligence.
[00:34 - 00:40] And lastly is sort of signing the definitive agreement and all the work that goes along with actually closing a deal.
[00:40 - 00:45] This takes anywhere from 16 to 36 weeks on average, but of course larger deals can take far longer than that.
[00:45 - 00:49] So let's look at those four periods in a little bit more detail.
[00:49 - 00:57] So in that first period, that preliminary assessment phase, from the perspective of the buyer and the investment bank,
[00:57 - 01:03] it's about reviewing the competitive landscape, working collaboratively to understand the landscape,
[01:03 - 01:08] to identify potential targets and to define some sort of rough acquisition timetable.
[01:08 - 01:15] And I've answered a little icons here that sort of designate that a lot of junior investment banking work
[01:15 - 01:18] is about sort of putting these kinds of documentations together.
[01:18 - 01:23] You saw a little bit of that, the pitch book represents really a very preliminary document.
[01:23 - 01:29] Once you get deeper into a deal where the buyer is actually expressed some interest in pursuing something,
[01:29 - 01:34] then you look at potential targets and we'll look at some pitch books like that in just a moment.
[01:34 - 01:39] The second period is about actually contacting targets and performing some valuation of targets.
[01:39 - 01:46] And so this is a process of contacting candidates, negotiating what's called a non-disclosure agreement or a confidentiality agreement,
[01:46 - 01:52] and performing some preliminary target evaluations based on the information that you get back from those targets.
[01:52 - 01:58] And that valuation work again represents the heart of what we've been talking about and what we're going to do in this course.
[01:58 - 02:00] It's about building an operating model for the target.
[02:00 - 02:03] It's about building a DCF model for that target.
[02:03 - 02:07] It's about spreading comps for the target, both trading and transaction comps.
[02:07 - 02:15] And lastly, it's about building a merger model that analyzes what happens when my company, the buyer, merges with the seller.
[02:15 - 02:17] What are the implications?
[02:17 - 02:21] What are those proformal financials or combined financials look like?
[02:21 - 02:29] Again, I've sort of noted that this is, again, a lot of junior bank work is putting these numbers together.
[02:29 - 02:33] The next period is about actually going through the deal.
[02:33 - 02:39] Presumably, if we've been able to narrow down a potential target, that target,
[02:39 - 02:47] or sometimes targets, receive an LOI, letters of intent from us, the buyer.
[02:47 - 02:50] At this point, more complete valuation analyses are done.
[02:50 - 02:58] This is fairly iterative, so these deal books are now being turned over pretty frequently as negotiations are taking place in different parameters or discussed.
[02:58 - 03:02] Business due diligence begins to get conducted, and that means you're doing,
[03:02 - 03:09] into continuing to do more and more in-depth industry analysis, as you're getting more and more information from the target company.
[03:09 - 03:12] You're asking for more information, you're getting more information.
[03:12 - 03:17] And so the due diligence is part of this overview of products and services, site visits.
[03:17 - 03:19] These are things that are happening.
[03:19 - 03:23] Some of these are more junior bank or have the other, are more senior bank heavier.
[03:23 - 03:28] Again, I've noted sort of where most of the junior bank work is taking place.
[03:28 - 03:34] Financial due diligence, you're reviewing typically a ton of documentation.
[03:34 - 03:36] Documents are coming in.
[03:36 - 03:41] You're looking at sort of how the company is doing on a more on an increasingly granular level.
[03:41 - 03:45] The final period is really the definitive agreement in closing.
[03:45 - 03:48] At this point, you're sort of finalizing the due diligence.
[03:48 - 03:52] You are negotiating the final definitive agreement.
[03:52 - 03:55] This is mostly the senior bankers.
[03:55 - 04:00] The lawyers are working directly with the clients at this point to sort of get the final touches.
[04:00 - 04:13] The role of the junior bankers are primarily about sort of turning over the underlying analysis to reflect what's being negotiated at this point by the buyer and the seller.
[04:13 - 04:14] Securing financing.
[04:14 - 04:21] So to the extent that you need to borrow money, that the buyer is going to need to borrow money in order to do this deal or issue stock to do this deal.
[04:21 - 04:25] There's going to be a whole bunch of things that need to get done.
[04:25 - 04:27] So required SEC filings.
[04:27 - 04:32] Shareholder and regulatory approvals, along with all the legal and tax work that goes along with that.
[04:32 - 04:38] And ideally, once all is said and done and everything is working well, the transaction closes.

### 7. MA-Modeling-Conceptually-Understanding-Contribution-Analysis

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-Conceptually-Understanding-Contribution-Analysis/transcript/MA-Modeling-Conceptually-Understanding-Contribution-Analysis.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-Conceptually-Understanding-Contribution-Analysis\transcript\MA-Modeling-Conceptually-Understanding-Contribution-Analysis.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:09] Our next modeling exercise is to perform what's called a contribution analysis as well as an implied target valuation based on contribution.
[00:09 - 00:18] One of the things that we can do now that we've completed our core model is to visualize the financial contributions of both firms to the combined entity.
[00:18 - 00:21] And so this is the result of a model like this.
[00:21 - 00:27] We can easily plug in the relative EBITDA revenue net income and enterprise value contributions.
[00:27 - 00:45] And what you see here for the Apple Disney deal that we just ran that revenue and EBITDA net income for Apple as a percentage of the total combined entity revenue EBITDA net income is roughly 80% and Disney contributes roughly 20%.
[00:45 - 00:54] But now notice how the enterprise value contribution for Apple is only 68% while Disney's contribution is 31% by the way.
[00:54 - 01:04] Notice that we're starting the graph just to make it a little bit easier visually as 50% so don't get confused as to why this part of the graph looks smaller even though it's 68%.
[01:04 - 01:12] This implies is that the enterprise value which represents the value of the operations as valued by the equity markets.
[01:12 - 01:21] We sort of back into an enterprise value by looking at equity value that's derived in the market and then adding back net debt.
[01:21 - 01:36] This sort of look at a purely from the perspective of its operating metric contribution for every dollar of revenue that Apple generates that dollar is valued less on a relative basis to Disney's dollar of revenue.
[01:36 - 01:46] And the same goes for EBITDA net income. Ultimately Disney seems to be valued. Each dollar of current Disney earnings seem to be valued more in the market than Apple.
[01:46 - 01:50] And this is probably somewhat very very familiar to you when we think about multiples.
[01:50 - 02:05] So when you look at enterprise value to revenue multiple this basically says that the Apple revenue multiple is lower than the Disney revenue multiple that the EBITDA multiple for Apple is lower than the Disney EBITDA multiple.
[02:05 - 02:09] This is expected that anyways because the deal is pretty diluted.
[02:09 - 02:17] So now taking that a step further you can actually perform what's called implied target valuation via contribution analysis.
[02:17 - 02:18] So what does that mean?
[02:18 - 02:25] So contribution analysis can be expanded to arrive at a target valuation.
[02:25 - 02:41] Let's look at this very simple fact pattern. We have let's say an acquire with expected revenue of $400 million a target with expected revenue of $100 million and we have an enterprise value assumption for the acquire of $800 million.
[02:41 - 02:46] Can we back into some sort of implied target enterprise value?
[02:46 - 02:54] The answer is if you assume that the target revenue is being valued the same way as acquire revenue.
[02:54 - 03:05] In other words if we just make the assumption sort of move away from the reality that companies have different multiples and let's apply the acquirers multiple effectively to the target this becomes very easy to do.
[03:05 - 03:10] So we can basically say okay well if they've got an enterprise value of $800 million.
[03:10 - 03:30] Let's use this revenue contribution the fact that 400 versus 100 means that the acquire would be contributing 80% of the revenue to the pro format entity right 400 divided by 400 plus 100 400 is 80% of now the combined revenue of 500.
[03:30 - 03:54] So that 80% compared to 20% means that if we look at valuation and just assume the acquirers valuation holds for the target then the enterprise value for the acquirer of $800 million we can assume that the combined enterprise value would be simply $800 million divided by 80% or $1 billion.
[03:54 - 04:12] And so this is pretty straightforward if you assume this is a major thing to assume but if you assume that and becomes $1 billion is the acquirer is the performer enterprise value and if the performer enterprise value is $1 billion and the acquirer enterprise value is $800 million.
[04:12 - 04:22] Well then backing into the target enterprise value sim just take the $1 billion minus the $800 million and you get to an implied target EV of $200 million.
[04:22 - 04:38] So this is actually an analysis that's quite common and it basically strives to answer if we ignore the fact that a dollar of revenue is valued differently by the target than it is for the acquirer then we can just go through this process.
[04:38 - 04:48] It's a central mechanism in this analysis and some of you may be wondering well what's the point obviously different companies have different reasons why they might be trading at different multiples and you'd be exactly correct.
[04:48 - 04:58] But the idea here is to strip away that to provide usually this is an acquirer centric analysis to provide the acquirer with some sort of context to the offer price that they're offering.
[04:58 - 05:08] And so we're going to take this step further obviously there's an offer price that it was being contemplated it does very much take into account the differences and potential growth prospect etc.
[05:08 - 05:19] But this provides some sort of benchmark that said hey if you ignore the potentially the you know when you're assuming that a target say like Disney might have higher growth prospects in Apple.
[05:19 - 05:29] you're making assumptions about the future if you strip away all those and just try to figure okay a dollar is a dollar whether it comes from the acquirer or the target what would that imply about the target valuation.
[05:29 - 05:39] So this process enables us to get to that so using the same fact pattern that we had we can now take it a step further and arrive at the implied target share price.
[05:39 - 05:49] And so if you have the same fact pattern the only other information that I need to give you to solve this is that the target shares outstanding in this exercise is 180 million.
[05:49 - 06:02] Target net debt is 20 million well we arrived at a 200 million dollar target enterprise value if it has net debt of 20 million the equity value is 180 million.
[06:02 - 06:15] And 180 million dollars in equity value given that the target has 180 million shares outstanding means that we're looking at an implied share price or value per share for the target is a dollar per share.
[06:15 - 06:28] And so whether or not you offered the target three dollars a share or two dollars a share this is what you should give it if you assume that a dollar is just a dollar and you don't try to impose any sort of growth expectations.
[06:28 - 06:31] That are different for the target than you do have for the acquirer.
[06:31 - 06:38] So that's the purpose of this analysis and in our next video we're going to go through the steps of modeling this out for the Apple Disney case study.
[06:38 - 06:48] It's analysis that appears alongside the accretion dilution month it's probably not as common as the accretion dilution month but it's increasingly common and so this is analysis we're going to in analysis the we're going to go through right now.

[00:00 - 00:07] In this video, we're going to walk through an exercise that firms your understanding of the concepts that we just went through.
[00:07 - 00:13] The same example that we've been working through from our prior example, that purchase price allocation exercise,

[00:15 - 00:22] Recall that the original discussion showed a fair market value of PPE going up to $400 million.

### 14. MA-Modeling-Fixed-vs-Floating-Exchange-Ratios-Part-1

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-Fixed-vs-Floating-Exchange-Ratios-Part-1/transcript/MA-Modeling-Fixed-vs-Floating-Exchange-Ratios-Part-1.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-Fixed-vs-Floating-Exchange-Ratios-Part-1\transcript\MA-Modeling-Fixed-vs-Floating-Exchange-Ratios-Part-1.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:04] In this video, we discussed exchange ratios.
[00:04 - 00:07] So, remember that in the M&A model,
[00:07 - 00:12] we calculated an exchange ratio of 1.14
[00:12 - 00:16] by looking at the offer price per share,
[00:16 - 00:19] divided by the acquire price per share,
[00:19 - 00:22] and the exchange ratio effectively says
[00:22 - 00:26] that I have to issue 1.14 Apple shares
[00:26 - 00:29] to buy one Disney share.
[00:29 - 00:33] Okay, and so this is what we assume
[00:33 - 00:37] the exchange ratio is going to be upon deal announcement.
[00:37 - 00:41] Now, the issue that exists with exchange ratios
[00:41 - 00:43] is that after the announcement,
[00:43 - 00:45] it could be several months before the deal closes.
[00:45 - 00:48] And what happens if Apple shares,
[00:48 - 00:50] if the Apple share price,
[00:50 - 00:52] or the Disney share price fluctuates
[00:52 - 00:54] to change that exchange ratio?
[00:54 - 00:56] The deal can be either structured,
[00:56 - 00:58] and most deals are structured where,
[00:58 - 01:01] no matter what, the exchange ratio per severes.
[01:01 - 01:04] It's going to be 1.14 Apple shares
[01:04 - 01:07] for one share of Disney, no matter what.
[01:07 - 01:10] But there is another not uncommon way to do,
[01:10 - 01:13] which is where this floats.
[01:13 - 01:15] In other words, if, for example,
[01:15 - 01:17] the Apple share price declines,
[01:17 - 01:19] you're going to have to issue,
[01:19 - 01:21] you're still issuing,
[01:21 - 01:24] you're going to have to issue more Apple shares
[01:24 - 01:27] in order to buy a Disney share.
[01:27 - 01:30] In other words, the exchange ratio starts to float
[01:30 - 01:32] as opposed to being fixed.
[01:32 - 01:35] And the question emerges about sort of
[01:35 - 01:38] who bears the risk associated with that.
[01:38 - 01:40] And is it better for the buyer,
[01:40 - 01:41] for the seller to structure the deal
[01:41 - 01:43] as a fixed exchange ratio?
[01:43 - 01:45] Like most mega deals are structured?
[01:45 - 01:47] Or a floating exchange ratio?
[01:47 - 01:49] Or what's called a fixed value?
[01:49 - 01:51] Floating exchange ratio really means you're offering a fixed value.
[01:51 - 01:54] So no matter what happens to relative share prices,
[01:54 - 01:56] you're offering a certain value to the target.
[01:56 - 01:58] And the way to wrap your head around this
[01:58 - 02:00] is with a simple sort of example,
[02:00 - 02:03] just taking the Apple and Disney scenario
[02:03 - 02:04] that we've been working on
[02:04 - 02:06] and running some numbers through it.
[02:06 - 02:08] So let's take a look at this.
[02:08 - 02:10] So if you recall, the Apple pre deal share price
[02:10 - 02:14] is $98.76, Disney pre deal, pre premium,
[02:14 - 02:16] or anything is $90.40.
[02:16 - 02:18] In that situation, the exchange ratio
[02:18 - 02:19] is actually less than one.
[02:19 - 02:22] Apple share price is higher than Disney,
[02:22 - 02:25] which means you have to issue less Apple shares
[02:25 - 02:27] than one in order to get one Disney share.
[02:27 - 02:29] You have to issue 0.9 Apple shares
[02:29 - 02:30] to get one Disney share.
[02:30 - 02:31] That makes sense.
[02:31 - 02:32] Well, upon announcement,
[02:32 - 02:34] we're announcing that hey,
[02:34 - 02:37] we are going to issue 1.14 Apple shares
[02:37 - 02:38] for each Disney share.
[02:38 - 02:40] Well, presumably that implies
[02:40 - 02:42] that Disney is worth $113,
[02:42 - 02:45] and that's why we're able to calculate that $113.
[02:45 - 02:47] Notice in our model, we actually hardcoded
[02:47 - 02:48] 113 via the premium,
[02:48 - 02:50] and we backed into the exchange ratio.
[02:50 - 02:52] But if, for example, in the press release,
[02:52 - 02:53] the company announces,
[02:53 - 02:56] hey, it's going to be 1.14 Apple shares for every Disney share,
[02:56 - 02:58] you can actually back into this.
[02:58 - 03:01] It's just sort of doing the math and reverse
[03:01 - 03:04] to get that 113.
[03:04 - 03:05] So with that,
[03:05 - 03:09] we now turn to what happens upon closing.
[03:09 - 03:11] So if you structure the deal,
[03:11 - 03:12] like I talked about,
[03:12 - 03:14] what's called a fixed exchange ratio,
[03:14 - 03:16] and again, remember most mega deals
[03:16 - 03:18] are structured with a fixed exchange ratio.
[03:18 - 03:19] It doesn't matter what happens
[03:19 - 03:22] to the acquire share price or the target share price.
[03:22 - 03:25] It's always going to be 1.14 target shares
[03:25 - 03:27] for each acquire share.
[03:27 - 03:28] That was the announcement.
[03:28 - 03:29] It's a fixed exchange.
[03:29 - 03:30] That's what it's going to be upon closing.
[03:30 - 03:32] So let's say that once the deal actually closes,
[03:32 - 03:35] several months after the announcement,
[03:35 - 03:37] Apple share price is $94.
[03:37 - 03:40] It's dipped by $5.
[03:40 - 03:42] Given the exchange ratio is fixed,
[03:42 - 03:44] you can back into what that implies
[03:44 - 03:47] about the closing share price for the target.
[03:47 - 03:50] That's simply going to be that 1.14 exchange ratio
[03:50 - 03:54] times the share price of $94 for Apple,
[03:54 - 03:57] and you back into the fixed exchange ratio.
[03:57 - 03:59] Nothing's changed.
[03:59 - 04:01] Now let's just run through
[04:01 - 04:04] what this implies about the deal value.
[04:04 - 04:08] So the pre-deal target share count was 1.7 billion.
[04:08 - 04:10] Recall that's coming from here.
[04:10 - 04:13] That's the fully diluted pre-deal share count.
[04:13 - 04:17] Now Apple is going to issue 2 billion shares
[04:17 - 04:21] to buy out those 1.7 billion shares.
[04:21 - 04:24] Apple has to issue 1.14 of its own shares
[04:24 - 04:28] for each one of these shares.
[04:28 - 04:33] That amount of shares at Apple's announcement price
[04:33 - 04:34] was $98.
[04:34 - 04:37] So keep in mind when this deal is announced,
[04:37 - 04:39] Disney's thinking to itself,
[04:39 - 04:42] great we're getting $199 billion worth of value
[04:42 - 04:45] because Apple shares are $98.76.
[04:45 - 04:47] But at closing they get far less.
[04:47 - 04:48] They get $10 billion less in value
[04:48 - 04:51] because it's a fixed exchange ratio
[04:51 - 04:54] and since the acquired closing share price was $94.
[04:54 - 04:57] Disney is now riding along the Apple train.
[04:57 - 05:00] They, whatever happens to Apple share price,
[05:00 - 05:02] Disney either suffers or benefits.
[05:02 - 05:04] In this case, the share price dropped.
[05:04 - 05:09] If the inverse happened and the Apple share price went up,
[05:09 - 05:11] well, they get a lot more value.
[05:11 - 05:14] And so the main takeaway is with a fixed exchange,
[05:14 - 05:17] Disney carries the risk of price movements
[05:17 - 05:19] for the acquire.
[05:19 - 05:24] A floating exchange ratio is just the opposite.
[05:24 - 05:28] No matter what happens to the acquired share price,
[05:28 - 05:31] Disney is always going to get $113 worth of value
[05:31 - 05:33] and the way that's going to be achieved
[05:33 - 05:35] is via either more Apple shares
[05:35 - 05:37] for each Disney share or fewer Apple shares
[05:37 - 05:38] for each Disney share.
[05:38 - 05:40] So let's run through the numbers here.
[05:40 - 05:44] So Apple closing share price is $94 just like
[05:44 - 05:46] in the example a moment ago.
[05:46 - 05:49] But this time, the closing share price for Disney
[05:49 - 05:51] is going to be $113 because share holders know
[05:51 - 05:53] that this floating exchange rate structure
[05:53 - 05:56] is going to give them $113 worth of value.
[05:56 - 05:58] Why?
[05:58 - 06:01] Well, because the exchange ratio is going to float up,
[06:01 - 06:04] Apple is going to now have to issue by virtue
[06:04 - 06:06] of how they structure this deal.

[00:00 - 00:07] Up to now, we've been alluding to the fact that whether you've structured a deal as a fixed exchange or a floating exchange,
[00:07 - 00:13] the parties are going to request some sort of protection to mitigate their own risks here.
[00:13 - 00:20] So in a fixed exchange ratio, the primary risk is borne by the target as the acquire share price may decline.
[00:20 - 00:24] You'll see that the target value that they ultimately receive can decline.
[00:24 - 00:30] And conversely, with a floating exchange or a fixed value, no matter what happens to the acquire share price,
[00:30 - 00:35] they have to issue more and more shares as their share price declines to keep the value the same.
[00:35 - 00:41] So acquirers typically are very concerned about floating exchange ratios and will request some sort of floor

[00:46 - 00:49] at a certain acquire share price.

[00:53 - 00:57] Presumably the target would also request some protections on the upside.

[01:01 - 01:06] the target would say, and this is typically the cases what's called a symmetrical floor and cap where they'd say,

### 17. MA-Modeling-Income-Statement-Adjustments-in-MA

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-Income-Statement-Adjustments-in-MA/transcript/MA-Modeling-Income-Statement-Adjustments-in-MA.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-Income-Statement-Adjustments-in-MA\transcript\MA-Modeling-Income-Statement-Adjustments-in-MA.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:03] Now that we've gone through a simple accretion dilution example,
[00:03 - 00:08] we can expand the exercise to start talking about some of the more granular adjustments
[00:08 - 00:13] that are going to need to be made in a more real or true-to-life accretion dilution model.
[00:13 - 00:16] So, we touched on this a little bit or at least alluded to it,
[00:16 - 00:18] but in the simple example that we just went through,
[00:18 - 00:21] we lumped the acquire and the target net incomes together,
[00:21 - 00:24] and in reality, before we can just lump the two net incomes together,
[00:24 - 00:27] there are several things that are sometimes significant that we have to do,
[00:27 - 00:32] we have to adjust to net income that's going to help in determining accretion dilution.
[00:32 - 00:35] In other words, even in a stock deal,
[00:35 - 00:39] there are some adjustments that we have to consider before we can just lump the two net incomes together.
[00:39 - 00:48] Those adjustments have to do with acquisition financing, synergies, fees, and accounting-related adjustments.
[00:48 - 00:53] So, now let's flesh out these other adjustments in a little bit more detail.
[00:53 - 00:56] So, with, in terms of acquisition financing,
[00:56 - 00:59] in our simple example where we assumed 100% stock deal,
[00:59 - 01:02] there was no real adjustment to the numerator recall,
[01:02 - 01:04] we just lumped the two net incomes together,
[01:04 - 01:06] and the big adjustment was on the denominator side,
[01:06 - 01:10] where we looked at the acquire's pre-deal share count,
[01:10 - 01:15] and then added the number of shares that would be required to buy out all the stock of the target.
[01:15 - 01:19] Well, in the case of 100% cash deal or a mixed deal,
[01:19 - 01:22] there's going to be some sort of financing required.
[01:22 - 01:27] So, either the acquire is going to use cash reserves that it has to make the acquisition,
[01:27 - 01:31] or the acquire is going to have to take on new debt to finance the acquisition.
[01:31 - 01:37] So, you can either sort of give the target cash by giving your own cash or borrowing money,
[01:37 - 01:41] and giving that cash that you just raised to the target.
[01:41 - 01:44] So, what that means is that on the numerator side now,
[01:44 - 01:51] there will be an adjustment, because the new borrowing impacts the income statement in the form of incremental interest expense.
[01:51 - 01:53] And if you're using existing cash reserves,
[01:53 - 01:58] so when you think of a company like Google or Facebook, these cash rich or Apple cash rich companies,
[01:58 - 02:01] when they're going to do their deal,
[02:01 - 02:08] if they spend cash rather than borrowing new money and having this incremental interest expense component on the income statement,
[02:08 - 02:15] you're going to have to reflect the fact that you're no longer generating interest income from the excess cash reserves that have gone to finance the acquisition.
[02:15 - 02:20] So, whether you use excess cash or whether you actually borrow money,
[02:20 - 02:26] the impact on the combined net incomes on the numerator is going to be a reduction.
[02:26 - 02:35] The major adjustment to EPS in a cash deal is almost always the incremental interest expense that arises from additional debt that's issued to finance the deal.
[02:35 - 02:40] The next adjustment is cost savings, synergies.
[02:40 - 02:46] We talked about both the revenue and the cost savings, potentially they can arise from synergies.
[02:46 - 02:51] The impact of that is almost always just positive to the combined net income,
[02:51 - 02:55] so you combine the acquire the target net income, plus the benefits of the synergies.
[02:55 - 02:59] Now, we're going to talk about the nuances of sort of calculating those synergies,
[02:59 - 03:03] so they're usually expressed in the form of pre-tax, forms where you have to tax effect them,
[03:03 - 03:10] and of course, revenue synergies also have an associated margin that needs to be applied to it in the modeling context.
[03:10 - 03:17] But, generally speaking, both cost savings and revenue incremental revenues that arise from acquisition,
[03:17 - 03:19] that's what's called the synergy.
[03:19 - 03:22] And that often represents the main rationale of an acquisition,
[03:22 - 03:28] and that's to make sort of significant cuts in expenses by eliminating overlapping R&D efforts, closing down plants,
[03:28 - 03:30] employee redundancies.
[03:30 - 03:34] Those are the kinds of things that we're going to model out in the form of synergies onto the income statement,
[03:34 - 03:39] as you'd imagine, the impact there is on the numerator as well, but it's a positive impact,
[03:39 - 03:42] as opposed to the financing, which is a negative impact.
[03:42 - 03:44] Next, let's turn to fees.
[03:44 - 03:50] So, invest on banking, advisory fees, the lawyers, legal fees, and the accountant's accounting fees,
[03:50 - 03:54] those need to be expensed as they are incurred on the income statement,
[03:54 - 03:57] so the accounting for those is fairly straightforward, and I would say intuitive.
[03:57 - 04:05] When you incur an expense, when you incur the fee, that is recognized immediately on the income statement,
[04:05 - 04:10] it's expensed as incurred, and thereby that's a reduction to proforma net income,
[04:10 - 04:13] and therefore also a reduction to proforma EPS.
[04:13 - 04:18] Now, underwriting fees are treated from an accounting standpoint, under US GAAP and IFRS,
[04:18 - 04:22] is distinct from the other transaction fees.
[04:22 - 04:26] So, when a company borrows debt to finance an acquisition,
[04:26 - 04:30] the fees related to this borrowing, and whenever you take on debt,
[04:30 - 04:34] there are actually some immediate fees associated with that financing.
[04:34 - 04:39] Think about points when you're taking on a mortgage on your house.
[04:39 - 04:42] There are upfront fees for actually borrowing money.
[04:42 - 04:47] So, the fees related to this borrowing are treated differently from advisory, legal, and accounting fees.
[04:47 - 04:49] Underwriting fees are capitalized.
[04:49 - 04:54] Rather than being expensed as incurred, like the investment banking and advisory fees,
[04:54 - 05:00] the underwriting fees are capitalized and then amortized over the life of the debtage issuance.
[05:00 - 05:06] This creates an incremental amortization expense, which reduces proforma net income over the term of the borrowing.
[05:06 - 05:15] Now, the big difference is, whereas these fees, if you've got a, say, a $5 million fee from a deal,
[05:15 - 05:23] that immediately reduces net income by $5 million, and presumably the cash goes down as the offset in credit.
[05:23 - 05:30] But if you have a $5 million financing fee, the way you treat it is, you have to assess,
[05:30 - 05:34] sort of, so let's put an arrow here, and you sort of have to ask yourself, what is the term of the loan?
[05:34 - 05:37] Let's say the loan is five years.
[05:37 - 05:44] So, the accounting for that is actually that you amortize it, so you capitalize it as an intangible asset.
[05:44 - 05:50] So, immediately on day one, when it's happening is, you know, cash goes down as you pay the fee.
[05:50 - 05:53] Cash goes down by $5 million.
[05:53 - 05:55] Let's sort of do this this way.
[05:55 - 06:01] Cash goes down by $5 million, and the corresponding entry is not retained earnings, but it's this intangible asset.
[06:01 - 06:05] Financing fee asset that goes up by $5 million.
[06:05 - 06:11] Perhaps somewhat counterintuitively, and then over the next five years, the asset is amortized.
[06:11 - 06:23] So, it goes down from five to four to three, and on and on while the corresponding impact there is a reduction to retain earnings via amortization expense on the income statement.
[06:23 - 06:32] And sort of the conceptual logic for this is under your scat the argument is, you want to match the benefits of the borrowing.
[06:32 - 06:39] You want to rather match the benefits of the fees with the bar over the term of the borrowing.
[06:39 - 06:45] In other words, when you're borrowing money, that's going to give you some benefits and enables you to do this acquisition.
[06:45 - 06:52] And it gives you the benefits over the term of the borrowing is the argument for amortizing over that period as opposed to expensing.
[06:53 - 07:04] Fazby has actually considered modifying the accounting treatment here so that it is so that it is expenses incurred as well to sort of bring these fees online.
[07:04 - 07:13] But as it stands now, this is the accounting treatment under any fees are capitalized and amortized to make sure that you understand the distinction.
[07:13 - 07:22] So, the very last sort of broad umbrella of adjustments are accounting adjustments and these are the trickiest.
[07:22 - 07:28] As we start building our model, you'll find that these are the ones that tend to be the most complicated to wrap your head around.
[07:28 - 07:36] They're non-cash related beyond sort of the accounting. There's nothing really economically happening here as a result of the acquisition.
[07:36 - 07:43] Let's talk about these broad adjustments. So, the big thing in an acquisition is all the incremental depreciation amortization,
[07:43 - 07:50] asset potential one-time asset write-ups or write-downs that occur as a result of the deal.
[07:50 - 07:54] So, bear in mind we're going to get into this in a lot more detail shortly.
[07:54 - 08:03] The target assets, like property plan equipment and intangible assets, that the target has on its balance sheet are written up to family market value during on deal day.
[08:03 - 08:14] Now, what that means is these amortizable and depreciable assets end up creating more depreciation amortization over time.
[08:14 - 08:20] If an asset has a higher value because you've marked it to family market value, now you're depreciating over the same useful life,
[08:20 - 08:24] all else equal you're going to have higher depreciation going forward.
[08:24 - 08:31] And what that means is that incremental depreciation amortization that wasn't there before just simply because you wrote it up to family market value.
[08:31 - 08:40] where you can't write up assets to most assets of family market value unless there's something like a change of control that would merit that would merit the write up.
[08:40 - 08:49] And so, all of a sudden you have this higher depreciation amortization which reduces proforma net income over what it would have been without a deal.
[08:49 - 08:53] Because of course, depreciation amortization reduces net income.
[08:53 - 09:03] The depreciation amortization is baked into cost of goods sold, SGA, other operating expense line items which reduces proforma net income and as a result proforma EPS.
[09:03 - 09:21] So, these accounting adjustments which are not really economic adjustments end up being diluted by themselves which is why we actually see a lot of times and as we'll go through in our exercise that a lot of analysts exclude the impact of this when analyzing the accretion or dilution.
[09:21 - 09:29] Accounting adjustment, there's one other accounting adjustment and that is a major accounting adjustment in most acquisitions and that's the creation of goodwill.
[09:29 - 09:38] So, goodwill created in an acquisition obviously can be pretty significant and certainly in our case it is going to be a significant amount of goodwill created in acquisition.
[09:38 - 09:42] We'll talk a lot about why that happens in the accounting that underlies it.
[09:42 - 09:50] But the main thing that we have to keep in mind from a accretion dilution standpoint is that goodwill does not really have any impact on the income statement.
[09:50 - 10:01] Certainly, at least not immediately, goodwill was amortized a long time ago, over 10 years ago the accounting was that goodwill would be amortized just like other intangible assets.
[10:01 - 10:03] But that hasn't been the case in over a decade.
[10:03 - 10:13] So, the accounting for goodwill is just stays on the books and the only way that goodwill flows through the income statement in any way is via an impairment.
[10:13 - 10:18] And certainly we don't forecast any impairments when we build an accretion dilution model.
[10:18 - 10:27] The sort of implicit assumption when we do an accretion dilution model is that this acquisition will not be a total disaster which is what a goodwill impairment implies.
[10:27 - 10:40] So, lastly, before we get into our sort of mini exercise, a slightly more complicated version of the accretion dilution exercise that we just went through but not quite yet a full blown accretion dilution exercise.
[10:40 - 10:46] The last thing I want to mention before we get into that is how accretion dilution is actually presented in practice.
[10:46 - 10:49] And usually it's presented using a data table in Excel.
[10:49 - 10:55] You see something like this where you have the, you know, peanut butter, acquires jelly.
[10:55 - 10:59] And in year one accretion dilution looks like this.
[10:59 - 11:05] So, at various sort of forms of consideration, if we sort of sensitize our model to show stock versus cash.
[11:05 - 11:13] And then the range of offer prices, what is the accretion dilution that you'd expect to see and what does that represent as a premium to the market.
[11:13 - 11:18] So, obviously if it's a sort of mostly cash deal, the deal becomes quite accretive.
[11:18 - 11:22] Obviously the lower the offer price, the more accretive it is to the acquire.
[11:22 - 11:26] That would make sense if you can get away with buying the same company for less.
[11:26 - 11:29] That's going to make your financials look better because you have to issue less stock.
[11:29 - 11:31] You have to borrow less money.
[11:31 - 11:39] And this represents the premium that you'd have to pay over the market price of the target to make the deal happen.
[11:39 - 11:46] Okay, now that we've talked about all the adjustments that typically make up in accretion dilution broadly speaking at least.
[11:46 - 11:54] In our next video, we turn to doing a simple exercise where we look at what would happen if Google was to acquire a LinkedIn from an accretion dilution standpoint.

[20:04 - 20:05] if they're stock involved.
[20:05 - 20:09] The more stock you have, and let me just kind of zoom out, and we talked about sort of
[20:09 - 20:12] the P ratios determining that dilution.
[20:12 - 20:17] So Google is in a situation where the more stock they offer, the more dilution is going
[20:17 - 20:18] to be.
[20:18 - 20:24] Now, if you do no stock, the deal is still pretty dilutive, and then it sort of regresses
[20:24 - 20:27] to the cost of borrowing.
[20:27 - 20:32] If the borrowing has a really low cost, like 1%, and you can imagine if Google uses its
[20:32 - 20:36] excess cash reserves, that's really the amount of cash for gun.
[20:36 - 20:37] You start getting a creation.
[20:37 - 20:41] If you could borrow a 2%, the deal is only marginally dilutive.
[20:41 - 20:43] So those are the primary drivers.
[20:43 - 20:47] If it's 100% stock deal, what's driving it is the P ratios.
[20:47 - 20:53] If it's a 0% stock, in other words, all cash, what's driving is the cost of the financing.
[20:53 - 20:55] All right, let's bring that back to normal.
[20:55 - 20:58] And so that's why it's actually quite helpful, and I'm going to bring this back to normal
[20:58 - 21:00] as well, or to our original, I should say, assumption.
[21:00 - 21:06] And that's why when you look at the data table, the data table sort of helps crystallize
[21:06 - 21:08] all of this.
[21:08 - 21:13] So a different percent stock considerations, you see that the deal gets incrementally
[21:13 - 21:16] more dilutive, and that's because the P ratio of Google is actually lower than that
[21:16 - 21:17] of LinkedIn.
[21:17 - 21:23] And of course, at lower offer prices, the lower the offer price, the lower the LinkedIn
[21:23 - 21:28] PE ratio, but it never gets low enough, at least reasonably based on their trading levels,
[21:28 - 21:29] to get to accretion.
[21:29 - 21:33] Of course, if you could buy LinkedIn for 120, which you couldn't, but then the deal becomes
[21:33 - 21:35] far less dilutive.
[21:35 - 21:42] If you could buy it, if it was trading at $80, it becomes basically break even.

### 19. MA-Modeling-MA-Accounting-Overview

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-MA-Accounting-Overview/transcript/MA-Modeling-MA-Accounting-Overview.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-MA-Accounting-Overview\transcript\MA-Modeling-MA-Accounting-Overview.transcript.txt`

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:10] Perhaps the most challenging part of building a merger model is understanding all the adjustments that occur because of the accounting rules associated with acquisitions.
[00:10 - 00:12] So first of all understand that
[00:12 - 00:22] FASB prescribes that we use what's called the acquisition method to account for acquisitions, and here we've included the rulings that govern the acquisition method.
[00:22 - 00:28] There's much alignment between US gap and IFRS, in other words, the accounting rules are generally quite similar.
[00:28 - 00:34] So everything that we go through here in this course is applicable both to IFRS and to US gap.
[00:34 - 00:49] And the main premise governing acquisition, the acquisition method is that the acquisition is viewed as the purchase of target assets in the assumption of its liabilities, all of which are written up or technically down to reflect their fair market values.
[00:49 - 00:55] Just bear in mind that writing up assets to fair market value is not something you can normally do.
[00:55 - 01:01] If a company sort of hums along, you can't just normally write up its assets to what you think they're worth.
[01:01 - 01:07] It's only in the context of an acquisition that you can write up things like PPE and intangible assets to reflect fair market values.
[01:07 - 01:15] And that's because of the conservatism principle and the historical cost principle, which says that if you're just operating a business, you can't just write up assets to whatever you think they're worth.
[01:15 - 01:22] But if there is a buyer that's now acquiring this business, you can write up the assets to their fair market value.
[01:22 - 01:45] The one other important critical piece of acquisition of the acquisition method is that if you purchase a company with a purchase price that's greater than the fair market value, though now written up value of all the assets and net of liabilities, that excess, that incremental purchase price is recognized as an accounting asset plug called Goodwill.
[01:45 - 01:48] The easiest way to make sense of this is with some examples.
[01:48 - 01:53] So let's just dive into the basic premise here.
[01:53 - 01:59] And sort of the allocation of that purchase price onto the assets is something called purchase price allocation.
[01:59 - 02:06] So purchase price allocation represents the process of getting from a pre deal target balance sheet to a post deal target balance sheet.
[02:06 - 02:14] And it actually assigns a sequence of steps that you have to go through in the process of allocating that purchase price.
[02:14 - 02:16] So what does that mean?
[02:16 - 02:20] Step one, allocate the purchase price to the target's tangible book value.
[02:20 - 02:32] And under US gap, the tangible book value is defined as actually all the assets that have all the liabilities excluding one specific asset, which is the target's pre existing Goodwill.
[02:32 - 02:36] Not the new Goodwill created in the deal, but any pre existing Goodwill.
[02:36 - 02:40] That's called tangible book value for the purposes of purchase price allocation.
[02:40 - 02:47] And then you write up the assets net of liabilities to fair market value.
[02:47 - 02:52] And if the purchase price is still greater than the fair market value, the excess is then allocated to Goodwill.
[02:52 - 02:54] Here's sort of a visual representation this.
[02:54 - 03:06] Let's say the tangible book value of the target company's $45 and the fair market value of the book value of all the assets net of the liabilities is 60.
[03:06 - 03:13] So we write up the book value by 15 million to get up to that 60.
[03:13 - 03:20] And now if we purchase the company, if the acquires purchase the company for 100, there's an excess of 40.
[03:20 - 03:28] The purchase price is about 40 greater than 40 dollars greater than the fair market value of the equity.
[03:28 - 03:31] And so that's the basic process.
[03:31 - 03:33] Now let's go through that.
[03:33 - 03:36] Actually before we go through that example, just a couple of thoughts here.
[03:36 - 03:43] Since many assets are commonly carried at below for market value in the target balance sheet, like PPE and Tangibles,
[03:43 - 03:44] LIFO inventory.
[03:44 - 03:48] If you're wondering why would LIFO inventory be carried below fair market value?
[03:48 - 03:50] LIFO is last in first out.
[03:50 - 03:58] That means that the remaining inventories are actually old in periods of rising prices, which is sort of typical.
[03:58 - 04:01] You're going to see inventories that are undervalued.
[04:01 - 04:10] And so an acquisition actually creates an event that merits the writing up of inventories under certain circumstances.
[04:10 - 04:12] PPE, of course, is probably a little bit more intuitive.
[04:12 - 04:17] You have things like buildings and land that have specifically land.
[04:17 - 04:24] You can imagine that is rising in value all this time and isn't being reflected up until the acquisition,
[04:24 - 04:27] same with intangible assets, brands, patents, things like that.
[04:27 - 04:32] They might be sitting grossly undervalued on the balance sheet and now get to be written up.
[04:32 - 04:35] So in just in terms of mechanics and an actual deal,
[04:35 - 04:40] you're not really going to know what the amount of write-ups are when you're doing the modeling.
[04:40 - 04:44] Actual appraisals are actually conducted at later stages of the deal.
[04:44 - 04:47] They often and often long after the initial analysis are required.
[04:47 - 04:53] So practitioners often just estimate the write-ups by applying some sort of industry percent
[04:53 - 04:55] or simply ignoring write-ups entirely,
[04:55 - 04:59] which is why a lot of times you'll have practitioners mistakenly tell you
[04:59 - 05:03] that goodwill represents the excess of the purchase price over the book value of assets
[05:03 - 05:07] as opposed to the excess of the purchase price over the fair market value of the assets.
[05:07 - 05:11] It's basically because they're ignoring the write-ups because they don't know what they are
[05:11 - 05:13] until later stages in the deal.
[05:13 - 05:15] Let's go through a simple exercise.
[05:15 - 05:19] It's going to make this a whole lot clearer, I think, for those that are seeing it for the first time.
[05:19 - 05:21] So here we've got a deal.
[05:21 - 05:24] The purchase price is one.
[05:24 - 05:26] And let's keep this in million.
[05:26 - 05:28] So you've got a billion dollar purchase price.
[05:28 - 05:33] So yeah, let's say millions just so we can keep the language consistent.
[05:33 - 05:35] So you've got a billion dollar purchase price.
[05:35 - 05:38] You have $400 million in fair market value of PP&E.
[05:38 - 05:40] And here is the pre-deal target balance sheet.
[05:40 - 05:44] So on day one, you're looking at a company has cash of $100 million.
[05:44 - 05:47] PP&E of $300 million that we know has a fair market value.
[05:47 - 05:50] appraisers come in, for example, and they determine it's not $300 million.
[05:50 - 05:51] It's $400 million.
[05:51 - 05:57] And you have some pre-existing goodwill from presumably prior acquisitions that the target had made,
[05:57 - 06:00] not the accord, but that the target had made and it's just sitting on the books at $50.
[06:00 - 06:03] You also have zero deferred tax liabilities.
[06:03 - 06:07] You'll see why we have to deal with that in subsequent videos.
[06:07 - 06:09] So you have zero right now, keep it simple.
[06:09 - 06:12] Debt of $50 and the remaining being the equity value.
[06:12 - 06:14] So assets equal liabilities and equity.
[06:14 - 06:18] And now, how do we apply the purchase price allocation sequence
[06:18 - 06:24] to get to what the new balance sheet for the target, what the new target balance sheet looks like?
[06:24 - 06:28] And again, ultimately the goal for us is to get from a pre-deal balance sheet

[06:37 - 06:43] And this is what we actually combine what we add to the acquire balance sheet when we build our model.
[06:43 - 06:48] So in order to build our model, we have to sort of apply the purchase price to the target balance sheet.
[06:48 - 06:51] So let's go through the sequence of steps that are prescribed by FASB.
[06:51 - 06:54] First we eliminate existing goodwill.
[06:54 - 07:04] So if we eliminate existing goodwill, we basically pretend like that doesn't exist.
[07:04 - 07:07] We get to a tangible book value.
[07:07 - 07:11] If we were to sort of stop right there, we would see that the tangible book value

[07:19 - 07:25] Now, FASB would be prescribed that because ultimately this is a accounting plug from prior acquisitions.
[07:25 - 07:28] We're trying to find out what the new goodwill is.
[07:28 - 07:32] So let's start from scratch and eliminate any prior accounting plugs.
[07:32 - 07:38] Presumably this will get wrapped into if this is still, you know, if prior acquisitions were worth whatever they were worth
[07:38 - 07:42] then the new purchase price will presumably reflect that and will capture that in the new goodwill.
[07:42 - 07:44] That's the sort of logic of the sequence of steps here.
[07:44 - 07:47] So that step one is accomplished.
[07:47 - 07:52] Again, the main takeaway here is that if you credit goodwill, you eliminate goodwill.
[07:52 - 07:54] There's a corresponding debit on the equity side.
[07:54 - 07:55] It's a step one.
[07:55 - 07:57] Step two is we adjust the fair market value.
[07:57 - 07:58] Well, here we're keeping it simple.
[07:58 - 08:04] The only asset that the company actually has other than cash or goodwill is PP&E.
[08:04 - 08:09] And that has a fair market value that's $100 million greater than the current carrying value.

[08:13 - 08:15] It's going to be a debit to PP&E.

### 26. MA-Modeling-Rule-of-Thumb-for-DTLs

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-Rule-of-Thumb-for-DTLs/transcript/MA-Modeling-Rule-of-Thumb-for-DTLs.transcript.txt`

**Full Path:** `c:\Users\manny\Documents\BUS696\Chapman_FSM_DCF\Videos\BUS_696_MA\MA-Modeling-Rule-of-Thumb-for-DTLs\transcript\MA-Modeling-Rule-of-Thumb-for-DTLs.transcript.txt`

---

Language: en (p=1.000)

[00:00 - 00:06] In the last video, you may have noticed that the initial defer tax liability that was created
[00:06 - 00:12] was simply the tax rate times the amount of that $100 million write up.
[00:12 - 00:13] It's not a coincidence.
[00:13 - 00:18] In fact, we can now formulate a general rule for calculating defer taxes in a stock sale.
[00:18 - 00:24] And that is that the detail created in an M&A stock sale is the fair value of the book
[00:24 - 00:28] basis minus the tax basis times the tax rate.
[00:28 - 00:30] You can see this visually.
[00:30 - 00:36] In fact, in every period going forward over the useful life of the asset that's creating
[00:36 - 00:43] the defer tax liability, you can calculate the end of period defer tax liability by simply
[00:43 - 00:45] looking at the difference in basis.
[00:45 - 00:51] So if the tax basis on deal days 300 in the book basis is 400, well, that $100 difference
[00:51 - 00:53] times the 40% tax rate is 40.
[00:53 - 00:56] That's the total amount of DTLs on the balance sheet.
[00:56 - 00:59] At the end of that balance sheet period where that difference is created.
[00:59 - 01:02] At the end of year one, the DTL goes down to 20.
[01:02 - 01:04] Remember, it went down from 40 to 20.
[01:04 - 01:08] And you can calculate it again by looking at the difference that in year one, once
[01:08 - 01:10] the assets have had a year's worth of depreciation.
[01:10 - 01:15] In our example, the book basis of the assets is 200 and the tax basis is 150.
[01:15 - 01:20] Meaning that $50 difference times the 40% tax rate is the remaining balance of the DTL.
[01:20 - 01:24] And then by the end of the period, sort of highlighting that this is in fact a temporary
[01:24 - 01:27] timing difference, we get to a DTL of zero.
[01:27 - 01:32] Since there is no difference times the 40% tax rate means the DTL was fully reversed.

### 34. MA-Modeling-The-Role-of-the-Banker-in-MA

**Relative Path:** `Chapman_FSM_DCF/Videos/BUS_696_MA/MA-Modeling-The-Role-of-the-Banker-in-MA/transcript/MA-Modeling-The-Role-of-the-Banker-in-MA.transcript.txt`

[00:00 - 00:03] Okay, let's get started.
[00:03 - 00:06] Before we can build a fully fledged M&A model,
[00:06 - 00:08] we need to understand some context.
[00:08 - 00:10] We need to understand the industry.
[00:10 - 00:12] Broadly speaking, when you're talking about mergers
[00:12 - 00:15] and acquisitions, you have the buyer and the seller.
[00:15 - 00:17] First, within these organizations,
[00:17 - 00:19] you have the business development teams,
[00:19 - 00:23] which are tasked with determining which acquisitions
[00:23 - 00:26] makes sense internally, defining strategy
[00:26 - 00:29] around potential acquisitions and partnerships.
[00:29 - 00:30] Then you actually have the management
[00:30 - 00:32] and the board of directors.
[00:32 - 00:35] And so these are the folks that are actually going to
[00:35 - 00:38] bring about and approve acquisitions.
[00:39 - 00:40] And lastly, you have the shareholders,
[00:40 - 00:42] which for deals of a certain size
[00:42 - 00:45] from a buyer's perspective require approval.
[00:45 - 00:48] And almost certainly from the seller's perspective,
[00:48 - 00:49] you're gonna need shareholder approval.
[00:49 - 00:51] So those are broadly speaking,
[00:51 - 00:53] the major groups that are involved in the M&A process
[00:53 - 00:55] within both buyer and seller organizations.
[00:55 - 00:57] There's also, of course, the functional groups,
[00:57 - 00:59] which are impacted by an acquisition,
[00:59 - 01:03] but these three groups, the business development folks,
[01:03 - 01:04] management and board and the shareholders,
[01:04 - 01:06] are really the ones that are actively involved
[01:06 - 01:07] in the acquisition from the get-come.
[01:07 - 01:12] On the other side of that, you have the supporting players.
[01:12 - 01:15] The investment bankers, and you have accounting,
[01:15 - 01:17] tax, and legal services.
[01:17 - 01:19] And lastly, when relevant, there's the regulator.
[01:19 - 01:22] So when you're talking about public deals,
[01:22 - 01:25] you're gonna have to comply with various types
[01:25 - 01:28] of disclosure requirements, regulatory requirements.
[01:28 - 01:31] There's also antitrust issues for certain deals.
[01:31 - 01:34] In terms of our course, as you probably imagine,
[01:34 - 01:38] the focus is gonna be on the invest in banking product,
[01:38 - 01:42] the actual technical analysis that underlies the M&A process.
[01:42 - 01:45] And obviously, you can't really disconnect the buyer
[01:45 - 01:46] and the seller from that process.
[01:46 - 01:48] So when you sort of think about the focus of this course,
[01:48 - 01:51] it's really predominantly on the buyer
[01:51 - 01:53] and the seller perspectives, the analysis that they have
[01:53 - 01:55] to go through as facilitated by the investment bank.
[01:55 - 01:59] The investment bank serves a very important role in M&A.
[01:59 - 02:02] Specifically, the investment bank provides advice
[02:02 - 02:06] on things like the optimal term and structure of a deal.
[02:06 - 02:07] It provides access to capital,
[02:07 - 02:10] or at the very least facilitates access to capital.
[02:10 - 02:14] And it also helps in many circumstances
[02:14 - 02:16] in actually finding buyers and sellers
[02:16 - 02:19] and even helping to negotiate the correct terms
[02:19 - 02:21] on behalf of either the buyer of the seller,
[02:21 - 02:23] depending on who the client is.
[02:23 - 02:26] Now, perhaps the three most common types of IB engagements,
[02:26 - 02:28] so from the perspective of the investment bank,
[02:28 - 02:31] the most common types of engagements are buy side engagements
[02:31 - 02:35] where your work is predominantly advising the buyer.
[02:35 - 02:37] There are sell side engagements
[02:37 - 02:40] where you're predominantly advising the seller.
[02:40 - 02:41] And lastly, there are fairness opinions.
[02:41 - 02:44] Fairness opinions are supposed to be,
[02:44 - 02:47] an independent opinion that speaks to the fairness of a deal.
[02:47 - 02:49] And it's a document that has a lot of the technical
[02:49 - 02:52] analysis that we're about to go through,
[02:52 - 02:54] and it's something that's provided to the board,
[02:54 - 02:56] presented to shareholders,
[02:56 - 02:59] and says, this is a deal that we think is fair.
[02:59 - 03:01] So one of the things to keep in mind
[03:01 - 03:03] in terms of the investment banking perspective
[03:03 - 03:08] is that work associated with potentially winning M&A
[03:09 - 03:12] business often begins long before deal is actually
[03:12 - 03:14] in play that the management teams on both sides
[03:14 - 03:16] have actually committed to doing a deal.
[03:16 - 03:19] And so through that process, and even before that process,
[03:19 - 03:22] investment banks are often pitching M&A ideas
[03:22 - 03:25] to both potential buyers and sellers.
[03:25 - 03:26] And so if you sort of think about
[03:26 - 03:29] what is the role of different professionals
[03:29 - 03:33] within the investment banking groups,
[03:33 - 03:37] well, the senior bankers are basically trying to win business.
[03:37 - 03:38] They're trying to bring business in,
[03:38 - 03:41] they're trying to cultivate relationships with management teams,
[03:41 - 03:43] trying to understand what the management teams are thinking,
[03:43 - 03:44] potential buyers and sellers,
[03:44 - 03:48] so that they can ultimately provide strategic advice
[03:48 - 03:49] that's gonna be relevant to the buyers and sellers.
[03:49 - 03:51] So they're cultivating relationships,
[03:51 - 03:52] trying to win business.
[03:52 - 03:54] The junior bankers are mostly tasked
[03:54 - 03:56] with doing the grunt work,
[03:56 - 03:59] the actual nuts and bolts analysis.
[03:59 - 04:03] And that represents the crux of what we're trying to do here,
[04:03 - 04:04] which is we're gonna walk you through
[04:04 - 04:06] how to actually build the analysis.
[04:06 - 04:08] You can sort of broadly look at that
[04:08 - 04:09] as being comprised of two components.
[04:09 - 04:13] One are the pitch books that are the presentation
[04:13 - 04:15] that the senior bankers are going to make,
[04:15 - 04:17] assuming that they can get the meeting
[04:17 - 04:19] to sort of pitch a certain idea
[04:19 - 04:21] or a strategic alternative.
[04:21 - 04:23] The modeling, what we're gonna go through
